Jamison Brereton

Indra (1), BrÃ•haspati (2, 4‚Äì8), Indra and
BrahmanÃ£aspati (3, 9), Indra and BrÃ•haspati (10)
Vasi·π£·π≠ha MaitrƒÅvaru·πái
10 verses: tri·π£·π≠ubh
According to the Anukrama·πáƒ´ (whose identifications are reproduced here in the heading) there is a wide array of divine dedicands for the individual verses of this hymn: Indra alone, BrÃ•haspati alone, Indra and BrÃ•haspati, and Indra and the more transparently named Brahma·πáaspati (‚ÄúLord of the Sacred Formulation‚Äù). The hymn is more carefully structured than the random listing would suggest, however.
First, as has often been discussed above, Hanns-Peter Schmidt (1968) has shown that BrÃ•haspati was originally an epithet of Indra in his priestly role and only gradu ally split off into a separate god. As in IV.50 this divine history is recapitulated in part in the progress of this hymn, though in a different way from the earlier hymn. For most of the hymn only one of the divine figures is on stage at a time: verse 1 has Indra, verse 2 BrÃ•haspati, and BrÃ•haspati holds sway in verses 4‚Äì8. Even in verse 3, supposedly dedicated to both Indra and Brahma·πáaspati, the two are not depicted together, and in fact the phraseology seems designed to identify Indra as Brahma·πáaspati: the two-word phrase br√°hma·πáas p√°tim ‚Äúlord of the sacred formula
tion‚Äù in pƒÅda b is, it seems, paraphrased with the defining relative clause of d, refer ring to Indra: yoÃÅ br√°hma·πáa·∏• …rƒÅÃÅjƒÅ ‚Äúwho is king of the sacred formulation,‚Äù with ‚Äúking‚Äù substituting for ‚Äúlord,‚Äù thus superimposing the one on the other. What we are claiming here, then, is that in verses 1‚Äì8 the divine names Indra and BrÃ•haspati/ Brahma·πáaspati refer to a single divine figure, Indra.
But when the transparent designation Brahma·πáaspati recurs in verse 9, it is immediately preceded by the dual pronoun vƒÅm ‚Äúyou two‚Äù; there must therefore now be two divine addressees, and the split between Indra and his epithet has been effected. The last two verses (9‚Äì10) call upon them both, using dual verbs, pro
nouns, and vocatives quite insistently. It is because we consider br√°hma·πáas p√°ti- an epithet in verse 3 and a divine name in verse 9 that we have translated them differ ently in the two verses. Notice also that Brahma·πáaspati, as opposed to BrÃ•haspati, is
only found in verses also containing the noun br√°hman ‚Äúsacred formulation.‚Äù But the hymn has another divine presence, not named but clearly there, who complicates the identity problem even more. The entire hymn is set on the ritual ground; this scene is set at the very beginning, in verse 1, starting with the word yaj√±√© ‚Äúat the sacrifice‚Äù and continuing with a clear depiction of the locus of ritual offerings, where men and gods (including Indra) meet. But, in the middle verses mentioning only BrÃ•haspati (4‚Äì8), that god is described in ways extremely suggestive of Agni, the ritual fire: he sits on the womb (vs. 4); he is both bright and roaring (vs. 5), bright, golden, and good to enter (vs. 7), and most clearly, in the image of verse 6 he is dark below but possessed of horses like ruddy clouds. All of this phraseol ogy has clear counterparts in descriptions of Agni. Thus, the divine figure Indra/

BrÃ•haspati is identified here in this ritual context with the ultimate ritually oriented god, Agni. This identification is found elsewhere; see especially V.43.12. It is noteworthy, but not surprising, that the usual mythological association of Indra/BrÃ•haspati with the Vala cave is entirely absent.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Indra and B·πõhaspati Renou treats this hymn in EVP XV.66-69. For the structure of the hymn and the covert identification of B·πõhaspati (/Indra) with Agni, see published introduction. This identification is argued for extensively by Schmidt (B·πõhaspati und Indra, 62-67, which also contains a complete tr. and philological comm.).

English translation:

‚ÄúAt the sacrifice in the dwellings of man upon earth, where the devout leaders of (rites) rejoice, wherethe libations to Indra are poured out, there may he descend before (other gods) from heaven for his exhilaration;(may his) swift (horses approach).‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

In the dwellings: at the sacrifice which is the home of the priests upon earth

Jamison Brereton

At the sacrifice, at the seat of men (coming) from heaven and from earth, where men seeking the gods become exhilarated,
where pressings are pressed for Indra, he will come at first for
exhilaration and to vitality.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This vs. plays on the ambiguity of reference of the noun n·πõÃÅ-, which can refer both to superior (mortal) men and to gods. It also cleverly but uninsistently identifies the sacrifice as the meeting place of men and gods, the n·πõ·π£√°dana- ‚Äòseat of men‚Äô who come from / belong to both heaven and earth (div√°·∏• ‚Ä¶ p·πõthivyƒÅÃÅ·∏•) – though see Geldner‚Äôs n. 1a for other, in my opinion less likely, possibilities. The n√°ra·∏• in pƒÅda b, however, seem only to be men proper, that is mortals, who seek the gods at the sacrificial common ground. 123 In c sunv√© is one of the rare exx. of a singular verb with neut. pl. subj. (here s√°vanƒÅni), a construction that is of course supported by comparative evidence. Grassmann interprets the verb rather as a 1st sg. transitive. This is not impossible – and note the 1st pl. verb in 2a – but sunve is otherwise passive, with 3rd pl. sunvir√© likewise passive.

In d the verb g√°man (in sandhi) could represent either 3rd pl. g√°man or 3rd sg.

g√°mat, but both the context, with Indra mentioned in the preceding pƒÅda, and the parallel I.178.2d g√°man na √≠ndra·∏• sakhyƒÅÃÅv√°ya≈õ ca support the 3rd sg.

The pƒÅda is also marked by case disharmony: dat. m√°dƒÅya and acc. v√°ya≈õ ca appear to be joint complements of g√°mat, conjoined by ca. Such case disharmony is rare in ca collocations (see Klein DGRV I.56-57), but at least in this example poses no obstacle to understanding: the dat. expresses purpose, the acc. goal. Although neither Klein nor I find the construction problematic, Renou supplies a second verb to govern v√°ya·∏• (‚Äúobtenir‚Äù), and HPS interprets the acc. as an Inhaltsakk.

A more problematic issue, at least for me, is the position of ca, unmentioned by any one, incl. Klein. The standard tr./interpr. take the 2nd term of the conjoined NP to be pratham√°·πÉ v√°ya·∏• ‚Äúfirst vitality/youth,‚Äù but we should then expect the ca to follow pratham√°m, the first word of the second member. Although such positioning is not an unbreakable rule, it is remarkably regular. To avoid the problem I take pratham√°m as an adverb here, as I do in the parallel I.83.4 (pratham√°m ‚Ä¶ v√°ya·∏• without a ca), cited by Oldenberg and Renou, for which see comm. ad loc.

Griffith

WHERE Heaven and Earth combine in men’s assembly, and ttose who love the Gods delight in worship,
Where the libations are effused for Indra, may he come first to drink and make him stronger.

Geldner

Zum Opfer, zu der M√§nnerversammlung des Himmels und der Erde, wo die gottergebenen M√§nner schwelgen, wo dem Indra Trankspenden gepre√üt werden, soll Indra kommen, um sich zu berauschen, und zur ersten Jugendkraft kommen.

Grassmann

Beim Himmelsfest, im M√§nnersitz der Erde, wo sich erfreun die gottergebnen M√§nner, Wo Opfertr√§nke ich dem Indra braue, da komm zum Rausch er, zu der ersten Labung.

English translation:

‚ÄúLet us solicit, friends divine protections, for B·πõhaspati accepts our (oblations); so may we be withoutoffence towards that showerer (of benefits) whois our benefactor from afar, as a father (of a son).‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

We choose divine help. BrÃ•haspati holds himself ready for us here, o comrades,
so that we might become without offense to the one who grants
rewards, who is a giver to us from afar, like a father.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The problem in this vs. is maha (sandhi form) in b b·πõÃÅhaspatir no maha ƒÅÃÅ sakhƒÅya·∏•. The Pp reads this as mahe, as do most subsequent interpr. – though maha·∏• is possible and is in fact the interpr. of at least one tr.: HPS takes it as the voc. pl. of m√°h-, construed with sakhƒÅya·∏•, hence ‚Äúihr grossen Freunde.‚Äù But this seems unlikely: there are no voc. forms to this stem in the RV (though the derived fem.

mahƒ´ÃÅ- does have some), and the intrusion of ƒÅÃÅin the middle of the voc. phrase seems unlikely. Others accept the Pp mahe and generally take it as a 3rd sg. verb, but opinions differ on its root affiliation and meaning. I will not detail these disagreements; see the disc. in Oldenberg, Geldner‚Äôs n. 2b, Renou ad loc., and Got≈ç 243-44. My interpr. is closest to Got≈ç‚Äôs: he assigns this to a root ‚àömah ‚Äòbring about‚Äô, separate from ‚àömah ‚Äòmagnify‚Äô, with a t-less 3rd sg. of the ≈õ√°ye type (see also comm. ad I.94.1) and tr. ‚ÄúB·πõhaspati ist f√ºr uns imstande.‚Äù I differ from him in the interpr. of the rest of the pƒÅda: he takes ƒÅÃÅas the trigger of an unexpressed verb of motion, ‚Äú[kommet] o Genossen herbei.‚Äù This seems to assume that the friends addressed are not coreferential with na·∏• earlier in the pƒÅda, or the referents of the 1st ps. verbs in pƒÅdas a and c. I do not entirely understand the position of ƒÅÃÅ, but it may show the occasional positioning of a preverb immediately after its verb or simply be an adverbial ‚Äòhere‚Äô, as in my tr.

As Renou points out, the optative (bh√°vema) is quite unusual in a y√°thƒÅ purpose cl., where the subjunctive is standard. See Grassmann s.v. y√°thƒÅ, cols. 1083-84, nos. 6-8. 124

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§â‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§π‡•á ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ! ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§â‡§™‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§∏‡§¨ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ò‡•ç‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§¨‡§ö‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§Ü‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§ø ‚Äú‡§∂‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§∂‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•Å‡§£‡§É ‡§∂‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•ã ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡•§ ‡§∂‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã ‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§∂‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§É‚Äù ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•Å‡§É ‡•©‡•¨‡•§‡•Ø‡•• ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‚Äò‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‚Äô ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡•®‡••

‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§∂‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∂‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§É ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§Ç ‡§§‡§Æ‡•Å ‡§§‡§Æ‡•á‡§µ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§Æ‡§∏‡§æ ‡§®‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§π‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•ã‡§°‡§æ‡§∂‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§≠‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§∏‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•É‡§£‡•Ä‡§∑‡•á ‡§ó‡•É‡§£‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§µ‡•á ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø ‡§ö ‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•à‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§É ‡§∂‡•ç‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï‡§É ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§É ‡§∏‡§ø‡§∑‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§∏‡•á‡§µ‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§É ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•à‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§§‡•É‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡§É ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§É ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡§ø ‡•§ ‡§§‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§¨‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§É ‡••

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúI glorify with homage and with oblations that most excellent and beneficent Brahma·πáaspati; may mypraise worthy of the deity, attain to the mighty Indra, who is the lord of the prayers offered by the devout.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Him, the preeminent, well-disposed Lord of the Sacred Formulation will I sing, with reverence, with oblations.
Let his divine signal-call mightily accompany Indra, who is the king of the god-created sacred formulation.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Both Geldner and Renou take great pains to avoid indentifying Indra in c with br√°hma·πáas p√°tim in b and the elaboration on this phrase in d, but as discussed in the published introduction. and extensively by HPS, the identification is the point.

Griffith

That Brahmanaspati, most High and Gracious, I glorify with offerings and with homage.
May the great song of praise divine, reach Indra who is the King of prayer the Gods’ creation.

Geldner

Ihn preise ich unter Verbeugung und Opferspenden als den H√∂chsten, den freundlichen Brahmanaspati-den Indra soll der g√∂ttliche Weckruf recht begleiten-der der K√∂nig des gottgeschaffenen Segenswortes ist.

Grassmann

Den hehren preis’ ich mit Gebet und Opfer, den holdgesinnten Sch√ºtzer des Gebetes, Zum Indra dring’ das hohe Lob der G√∂tter, zum K√∂nig des Gebets, des gottgeweihten.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúMay that best beloved B·πõhaspati, who is the desired of all sit down in our hall of sacrifice; may hegratify our desire of riches and of male posterity. Transporting us (at present) embarrassed uninjured beyond(the assaults of enemies).‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

Let him sit as the dearest in our womb here‚ÄîBrÃ•haspati who is
all-desirable [/granting all wishes].
Desire for wealth in good heroes‚Äîthat will he give. He will carry us
across the parched places unharmed.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The second pƒÅda contains an equational rel. cl. with expressed copula √°sti; main cl. equational expressions almost always lack copula (when asti is found, it is generally existential), but overt copulas are not uncommon in dependent clauses. It is of course optional; see the nominal rel. cl. in the preceding vs., 3d, which lacks copula.

PƒÅda c contains a phrase in the nominative, kƒÅÃÅmo rƒÅy√°·∏• suvƒ´ÃÅryasya ‚Äúdesire for wealth in good heroes,‚Äù which is picked up abruptly by the acc. prn. t√°m, object of the immediately following verb dƒÅt. There seems no other way to interpret it – and it goes perhaps too easily into English – but both the syntax and sense are slightly off.

The fronted expression seems like a topicalized phrase, but in Vedic topics would not default to the nominative but remain in the appropriate case for the larger syntactic frame; see in the next vs. the acc. phrase that occupies the whole of pƒÅda a, which is the obj. of the verb in b. Moreover, one doesn‚Äôt give wishes/desires but rather the contents of those desires, so that the referent of t√°m may be ray√≠-, not kƒÅÃÅma-. Both concerns suggest that the relationship between the kƒÅÃÅma- phrase and the abbreviated t√°·πÉ dƒÅt clause is less close than it appears. Renou supplies some structure to the first phrase – ‚Äú(En nous est) le d√©sir ‚Ä¶‚Äù – and something like that might produce the necessary distance.

Griffith

May that Brhaspati who brings all blessings, most dearly loved, be seated by our altar.
Heroes and wealth we crave; may he bestow them, and bear us safe beyond the men who vex us.

English translation:

‚ÄúMay the first-born immortals (by his command) bestow upon us the food that is necessary for existence;let us invoke the unresisted B·πõhaspati, to whom pure praises are addressed, he adored of householder.‚Äù

Let us invoke Brhaspati, the foeless, the clear-voiced God, the Holy One of households

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúMay his powerful brilliant horses, wearing a lustrous form like (that of) the sun, acting together, bring(hither) that B·πõhaspai, in whom strength abides like that of a substantial mansion.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

In whom strength abides: or,to whom belongs strenght, and whose friendship bestows a dwelling

Jamison Brereton

The capable, ruddy horses who pull together are pulling BrÃ•haspati, who is simply strength itself, whose seat has darkness while (the horses) like a cloud clothe themselves in ruddy form.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The construction of the vs. is uncertain in several regards, which center on the 2nd hemistich. The first is whether neut. s√°ha·∏• belongs in the rel. cl. or not; the position of rel. y√°sya is compatible with either answer. I take it as an independent qualifier of acc. b·πõÃÅhasp√°tim in b, hence an acc.: B·πõhaspati is identified with the abstract noun ‚Äòstrength / force‚Äô itself. I therefore assume that the rel. cl. begins with y√°sya. This also seems to be the Geldner solution. The sense of Renou‚Äôs tr. is similar, but he puts s√°ha·∏• in the, or a, rel. cl. as a nominative – taking c as containing two nominal rel. clauses: ‚Äúlui dont la force-dominante (est r√©elle, dont) le s√©jour-commun (est) noir.‚Äù HPS makes s√°ha·∏• the subject of an equational rel. cl.: ‚Äúdessen Gewalt eine schwarze St√§tte ist.‚Äù Since I think it more likely that B·πõhaspati is identified as strength itself than that his seat is, I find Schmidt‚Äôs interpr. less likely, though it does have the merit of not inserting a syntactic break in the middle of a pƒÅda. If B·πõhaspati is identified with s√°ha·∏• here (as I think), Schmidt‚Äôs claim that B·πõhaspati is identified with Agni in this hymn – an identification esp. clear in this vs. (see published introduction.) – is strenthened, since Agni is so often called ‚Äúson of strength‚Äù (s≈´n√∫- s√°hasa·∏•, e.g., in this ma·πá·∏çala VII.1.21, 22, 3.8, etc.). 125 A more interesting question is what to do with d. The pl. v√°sƒÅnƒÅ·∏• is universally, and plausibly, taken as referring to to the horses of ab: in pƒÅda they are called ‚Äòruddy‚Äô (aru·π£ƒÅÃÅsa·∏•); in d they ‚Äúclothe themselves in ruddy form‚Äù (r≈´p√°m aru·π£√°m). The question then is whether d is simply a continuation of the main cl. in ab, the part. v√°sƒÅnƒÅ·∏• modifying √°≈õvƒÅ·∏• in pƒÅda a, with the rel. cl. of c embedded in it.

This is perfectly possible and seems to be the standard interpr. Although we prefer to avoid interpr. with embedded relatives, once again the rel. cl. in this instance is nominal (whichever finer grained interpr. we follow), and nominal relatives are systematic exceptions to this rule. However, I prefer to take d as a continuation of the rel. cl. introduced by y√°sya, with oppositional nominal expressions, contrasting B·πõhaspati‚Äôs dark seat with his horses which take on ‚Äúruddy form.‚Äù This interpr.

allows the ‚Äòruddy‚Äô in d to be more than a pleonastic+++(=redundant)+++ repetition of the same word in pƒÅda a and gives more punch to the nƒ´ÃÅlavat sadh√°stham of c by making it part of a contrastive pair. If this interpr. is correct, the part. v√°sƒÅnƒÅ·∏• would be predicated.

Geldner (n. 6bc) notes the word play between semantically and etymologically distinct saha- (b) and s√°ha·∏• (c). In fact the play is more tightly constructed than he indicates, with the chiastic figure saha-vƒÅÃÅho vahanti / s√°ha·∏•, with the hemistich boundary isolating the semantically non-conforming word.

Griffith

Him, this Brhaspati, his red-hued horses, drawing together, full of strength, bring hither.
Robed in red colour like the cloud, they carry the Lord of Might whose friendship gives a dwelling.

Geldner

Diesen Brihaspati fahren t√ºchtige, r√∂tliche Rosse gemeinsam ziehend, das gar gewaltige Wesen, dessen Sitz dunkelfarbig ist, die Rosse die sich gleich der Wolke in r√∂tliche Farbe kleiden.

Grassmann

Brihaspati, ihn, dessen Kraft erstarkt ist, ihn fahren hin zum dunkeln Sitz die Hengste, Die starken, rothen, gleichen Wagen ziehend, mit rothem Glanz umkleidet, wie mit Wolken.

Elizarenkova

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§â‡§∏ (‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ú‡•ã (‡§∏‡§ß‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§®‡§ø‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•à (‡§®‡§≠‡§É) ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§ï‡§æ‡§∂ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï ‡§π‡•à, (‡§®, ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã‡§à ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§®‡§π‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§π‡•à, ‡§â‡§∏ (‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§É) ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•Å‡§à (‡§∂‡§ó‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏‡§É) ‡§Ü‡§®‡§®‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§≠‡§µ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä (‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§∏‡§É) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§™‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø‡§£ (‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§É) ‡§∂‡•Ä‡§ò‡•ç‡§∞‡§ó‡§§‡§ø‡§∂‡•Ä‡§≤ (‡§∏‡§π‡§µ‡§æ‡§π‡§É) ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§ú‡•ã‡•ú‡§®‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§≤‡•Ä ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Å (‡§µ‡§π‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø) ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ (‡§∏‡§π‡§É, ‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç) ‡§¨‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™ ‡§π‡•à ‡§î‡§∞ (‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø, ‡§®‡•Ä‡§≥‡§µ‡§§‡•ç) ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§®‡•Ä‡§° ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§ò‡•ã‡§Ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§π‡•à‡••‡•¨‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§£, ‡§Æ‡§®‡§®, ‡§®‡§ø‡§¶‡§ø‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§∏‡§æ‡§ß‡§®‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§à ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§É‡§ï‡§∞‡§£ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Å ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∂‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§™‡§ï ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶, ‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂, ‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™, ‡§∞‡§∏, ‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§ß ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§ ‡§π‡•à ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•ã‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•ã‡§ü‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡•á ‡§è‡§ï‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ò‡•ã‡§Ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§è‡§ï ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§è‡§ï ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§§‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡§æ‡§≤ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡•ã ‚Äú‡§è‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ã ‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§É‡•§ ‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§¶‡§ø‡§µ‡§ø‚Äù ‡••‡§ã. ‡•ß‡•¶‡•§‡•Ø‡•¶‡•§‡•©‡••‡•§ ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ã‡§ü‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§ï‡•ã‡§ü‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡•ç‡§° ‡§â‡§∏ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§è‡§ï‡§¶‡•á‡§∂ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ò‡•ã‡§Ç‡§∏‡§≤‡•á ‡§ï‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Ö‡§•‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§π‡•ã ‡§ï‡§ø ‚Äú‡§Ø‡•Å‡§û‡•ç‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§ß‡•ç‡§®‡§Æ‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡•Å‡§∑‡§É; BrÃ•haspati‚Äîgood (for libations) to enter, lofty, the best at making the pressed drink in abundance for his comrades.

Jamison Brereton Notes

It is difficult to wring a causal sense out of h√≠ here. The vs. continues the depiction of B·πõhaspati as Agni: the hundred feathers of the preening bird are the flames dipping and rising much like the action of preening; the golden axe or axes are likewise flames; while the descriptions in cd are focused on the role of Agni in the ritual.

On ≈õundhy√∫- see comm. ad V.52.9.

I would now be inclined to tr. the bahuvrƒ´hi h√≠ra·πáya-vƒÅ≈õƒ´·∏• (for the inflection see AiG II.2.408) as implicitly pl. (‚Äòhaving golden axes‚Äô, rather than the published

Wilson

English translation:

‚ÄúThe divine heaven and earth, the genitive ratrices of the deity have by their might, given growth toB·πõhaspati; magnify, friends, the magnifiable, and may he render (the waters) easy to be crossed and forded for(the attainment of) food.‚Äù

Jamison Brereton

The two goddesses, the world-halves, begetters of the god, strengthened BrÃ•haspati with their greatness.
Comrades, exert your skill for the one whose skill is to be besought. He will make good fords, easy to cross, for the sacred formulation.

Jamison Brereton Notes

I take the ‚Äòcomrades‚Äô addressed by the voc. sakhƒÅya·∏• to be different from those mentioned in the dative in the preceding vs. There the comrades of the god were the (other) gods who receive the oblation from Agni; here they are the 126 comrades of the poet, who urges them to tend to the god. The identity of comrades obviously depends on who they are comrades to.

Griffith

Both Heaven and Earth, divine, the Deity’s Parents, have made Brhaspati increase in grandeur.
Glorify him, O friends, who merits glory: may he give prayer fair way and easy passage.

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï ‡§ï‡•á ‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§§‡§ï ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø ‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï ‡§â‡§∏‡§ï‡•Ä ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¨‡•ã‡§ß‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‚Äò‡§ú‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‚Äô ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ø‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§á‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ (‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡§ü) ‡§ï‡§∞‡§§‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§ú‡•ã ‡§Ø‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ø‡§£‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§®‡•á ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§µ‡§π ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§æ ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§æ‡§∂‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ç‡§ï‡§ø ‡§á‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ü ‡§ö‡•Å‡§ï‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç, ‡§´‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§Ø‡§π‡§æ‡§Å ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§® ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§ï‡•à‡§∏‡•á ? ‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§™‡§¶‡•á‡§∂‡§®‡§ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§≤‡§∞ ‡§≠‡§ü‡•ç‡§ü, ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§ü‡§∞ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≤‡•ç‡§∏‡§® ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§´‡§ø‡§• ‡§∏‡§æ‡§π‡§¨ ‡§®‡•á ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§Ø‡§π‡•Ä ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§ø ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï ‡§î‡§∞ ‡§™‡•É‡§•‡§ø‡§µ‡•Ä‡§≤‡•ã‡§ï ‡§®‡•á ‡§¨‡•É‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§™‡•à‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡•§ ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§µ‡•à‡§¶‡§ø‡§ï ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§∏‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§•‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∞‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§π‡•à, ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡•• ‡§á‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§¨‡§≤‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï ‡§ñ‡§£‡•ç‡§°‡§® ‡§π‡§Æ ‡§®‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡§®‡§≤‡§ø‡§ñ‡§ø‡§§ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•á‡§Ç‡§ó‡•á ‡••‡•Æ‡••

‡§π‡•á ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡•Å‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•á ‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡§§‡•á ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ö ‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§ö‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•Ä ‡•§ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§∞‡•Ç‡§™‡§æ ‡§á‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§∏‡•Å‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ö‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø ‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§§‡•å ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Ç ‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§§‡§•‡§æ ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§Ç‡§ß‡•Ä‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§ß‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§¨‡§π‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡•Ä‡§É ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ó‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§ó‡§ø‡§∞‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§ ‡§∂‡•É‡§£‡•Å‡§§‡§Æ‡§ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§§‡•ç ‡•§ ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§≠‡§ø‡§ó‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§É ‡§µ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•Ñ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä‡§É ‡§∂‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§∏‡•á‡§®‡§æ‡§É ‡§ú‡§ú‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§â‡§™‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§™‡§Ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••

English translation:

‚ÄúThis praise has been offered as prayer to you both, Brahma·πáaspati and Indra, the wielder of thethunderbolt; protect our ceremonies; hear our manifold praise; annihilate the assailing adversaries of yourworshippers.‚Äù

Commentary by SƒÅya·πáa: ·πögveda-bhƒÅ·π£ya

Prayer: iyam suviktir brahma = iyam mantrar≈´pa stuti·∏•, this praise in the form of a mantra, asacred text or prayer

Jamison Brereton

Here is a well-twisted (hymn) for you two, o Brahma·πáaspati. A sacred formulation has been made for Indra, the mace-bearer.
Aid our insightful thoughts; awaken plentiful gifts. Exhaust the
hostilities of the stranger, of the rapacious ones.

Griffith

This, Brahmanaspati, is your laudation prayer hath been made to thunderwielding Indra.
Favour our songs, wake up our thought and spirit: destroy the godless and our foemen’s malice.

Geldner

Dieser Lobpreis ist f√ºr euch beide, o Brahmanaspati; dem Indra mit der Keule ward eine Erbauung bereitet. Beg√ºnstigt die frommen Gedanken, wecket die Freigebigkeit; d√§mpfet die Mi√ügunst des Nebenbuhlers, der Eifers√ºchtigen!

Grassmann

Dies reine Lied ist euch bereitet beiden, Brihaspati und Indra, der den Blitz h√§lt; Erweckt die Andacht, f√∂rdert die Gebete, vertilgt der Streiter opferlose Feinde.

‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§Ö‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§´‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§Ø‡§π ‡§ï‡§π‡§æ ‡§ï‡§ø ‚Äò‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§ß‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‚Äô ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§´‡§ø‡§∞ ‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§™‡§¶‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡•á) ‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§™‡§§‡•á ! (‡§µ‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§§‡§µ (‡§á‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§á‡§Ø‡§Ç (‡§∏‡•Å‡§µ‡•É‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§É) ‡§¶‡•ã‡§∑‡§∞‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å‡§§‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ø‡§æ (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ, ‡§á‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø) ‡§ê‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§§‡•á ‡§≠‡§µ‡§§‡•á (‡§µ‡§ú‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§£‡•á) ‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§®‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø (‡§Ö‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§ø) ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§æ, ‡§∏‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•Å, ‡§§‡§•‡§æ (‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ó‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç, ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡•Ä‡§É) ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡•Ä‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡•Å ‡§§‡§•‡§æ (‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É) ‡§à‡§∂‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡§É (‡§µ‡§®‡•Å‡§∑‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§®‡§É (‡§Ö‡§∞‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä‡§É) ‡§∂‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç (‡§ú‡§ú‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç) ‡§Ö‡§™‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§Ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••‡•Ø‡••

‡§Ü‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§®‡§ø - ‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É

‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§æ‡§É - ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§â‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≤‡§ø‡§Ø‡•á ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§π‡•Å‡§Ü ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ú‡§ø‡§∏‡§ï‡§æ ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§® ‡§á‡§∏ ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§§ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§ï‡§à ‡§è‡§ï ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§•‡§Æ ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§Ü ‡§ö‡•Å‡§ï‡§æ ‡§π‡•à‡•§‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§π‡•à‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§∏‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•á‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§¶‡§æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§è‡§ï‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§•‡§æ, ‡§á‡§∏‡•Ä ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•á ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡•ã (‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ) ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§•‡§® ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ó‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à ‡••‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§™‡§ø ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•á ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡•á ‡§≠‡•Ä ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§π‡•à, ‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§π‡•Ä ‡§π‡•à, ‡§ú‡•à‡§∏‡§æ ‡§ï‡§ø ‚Äú‡§§‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§®‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡•Å ‡§ö‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§Æ‡§æ‡§É‡•§ ‡§§‡§¶‡•á‡§µ ‡§∂‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§§‡§¶‡•ç‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‚Äù‡•§ (‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡•ç‡§†) ‡§∏‡§¨‡§∏‡•á ‡§¨‡•ú‡§æ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§ï‡§π ‡§ï‡§∞ ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§∂‡§¨‡•ç‡§¶ ‡§ï‡•ã ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡§ï ‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§π‡•à‡•§ ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç Griffith

Ye Twain are Lords of wealth in earth and heaven, thou, O Brhaspati, and thou, O Indra.
Mean though he be, give wealth to him who lauds you. Preserve us evermore, ye Gods, with blessings.
